The current board state shows White's King on g4 and Black's Rook on f8. Black has a pawn on f7. Moving the pawn to f5 (`f7f5`) gives check to the White King on g4. This move also opens the f-file for the Black Rook.

If White captures the pawn with the Queen (`Qxf5`), Black can recapture with the Rook (`Rxf5`), winning the Queen.
If White captures the Rook (`Qxf8`), Black can recapture with the King (`Kxf8`), trading a Rook for a Queen, which is favorable for Black.
If White moves the King, Black maintains the initiative with an open f-file and an advanced pawn.

Therefore, `f7f5` is the strongest move.

make_move f7f5

Player_Black (to Proxy):



The position is quite unusual with the White King exposed on f5 and the Black Queen on f1, supported by a pawn on f3. The Black Queen is currently blocked by the pawn on f3 from attacking the White King directly along the f-file.

The best move is to reposition the Queen to a more active square that supports the advanced pawn and restricts the White King.

**Analysis of candidate moves:**
1.  **`f1g2`**: This moves the Queen to g2. From g2, the Queen protects the pawn on f3 (diagonally), covers the g-file (preventing the King from escaping to g5, g6), and controls e4. It also protects the promotion square f1 and the square f2, paving the way for the pawn to advance to f2 and then promote. This seems like the most solid and winning plan.
2.  **`f1h3+`**: This delivers a check. However, the White King has several escape squares (e5, f6, g6, g5, f4). It doesn't seem to lead to a forced mate or significant advantage compared to the quiet maneuver.
3.  **`f1d3+`**: This also delivers a check. The King can escape to f4. It doesn't seem to restrict the King as effectively as `f1g2`.
4.  **`f3f2`**: Advancing the pawn blocks the Queen completely on f1, making the Queen passive. This is likely not optimal.

Therefore, `f1g2` is the best move. It improves the Queen's position, secures the pawn, and prepares for promotion.

make_move f1g2

Player_Black (to Proxy):



The current position shows the White King at d6, the Black King at g4, and the Black Queen at h1. It is Black's turn to move.

The goal is to checkmate the White King. The White King is in the center of the board. The Black Queen is on the long diagonal h1-a8.

Let's analyze the candidate moves:
- `h1d5`: This move places the Queen on d5, checking the King at d6. However, the Queen on d5 is not protected by the Black King at g4. The White King can simply capture the Queen (`Kxd5`). This is a blunder.
- `h1c6`: This move places the Queen on c6, checking the King at d6. Similarly, the Queen on c6 is not protected by the Black King. The White King can capture (`Kxc6`). This is also a blunder.
- `h1h6`: This move places the Queen on h6, checking the King along the 6th rank. The Queen is safe from capture. However, the King has several escape squares (c7, d7, e7, etc.).
- `h1d1`: This move places the Queen on d1, checking the King along the d-file. Crucially, the Queen on d1 is protected by the Black King at g4 (via the diagonal g4-f3-e2-d1). This is a safe check. It forces the White King to move and centralizes the Black Queen.

Comparing `h1h6` and `h1d1`, `h1d1` is superior because the Queen is protected, preventing any immediate loss of material. It also controls the d-file, which is directly in front of the White King.

Therefore, the best move is `h1d1`.

make_move h1d1

Player_Black (to Proxy):



The board shows that Black is down material (White has an extra Bishop and Knight), but Black has a very active Bishop on f2 that is annoying the White King on e2. The White King is exposed, and Black's Bishop is currently safe but could become a target or get trapped if not careful.

Analyzing the position:
- **Black Bishop on f2**: It is attacking e1 and g1 (Knight). It controls the long diagonal (blocked) and the h4-d8 diagonal.
- **White King on e2**: Exposed.
- **White defenses**: The King is defended by surrounding pieces, but the position is tight.
- **Threats**: White's Bishop on c1 is blocked by the pawn on e3, so it doesn't attack g5. The pawn on g5 is safe.
- **Candidate Moves**:
    - `f2g3`: Moves the Bishop to a safe, active square. It controls f4, h2, and e1. It is not attacked by any White piece (pawns on h3/g4 don't attack g3; bishops/knights/rooks don't attack g3). It keeps the pressure on the White King's position.
    - `f2h4`: Also safe, but less central and controls fewer squares.
    - `f2xg1`: Captures the Knight, but allows `Rxg1`, trading Black's active Bishop for a passive Knight, which is unfavorable given Black is already down material.
    - `f2xe3`: Captures a pawn, but allows `Kxe3`, losing the Bishop for a pawn.
    - Pawn moves (`a6`, `b6`, `h6`): Too passive.
    - Rook moves: Blocked or passive.

The move `f2g3` seems the most principled. It improves the Bishop's position, maintains activity, and keeps the White King under pressure without losing material.

make_move f2g3

Player_Black (to Proxy):



The position shows Black with a strong passed pawn on e3, which is one step away from the 2nd rank. However, the pawn is currently unsupported and would be vulnerable to capture by the White Knight on g1 if pushed to e2. The White Rook on a3 appears to attack the e-file, but its path is blocked by its own pieces (Pawn on b3 and King on c3), so it does not currently threaten the pawn on e3.

To secure the advance of the e-pawn, Black needs to support the e2 square. Moving a Rook to e8 will defend the pawn if it advances to e2, and also allow Black to recapture if the White Knight takes the pawn on e2.

The move `f8e8` places the Rook on the e-file, supporting the e3 pawn's advance to e2. If White captures the pawn on e2 with the Knight (`Nxe2`), Black can recapture with the Rook (`Rxe2`), winning a piece. If White ignores the threat, Black will promote the pawn on the next turn.

The move `a8e8` would also work, but `f8e8` is a direct and effective choice.

make_move f8e8

The position features a Black pawn on e3, supported by a Rook on e8. The White King is on c3 and the White Bishop is on c2. The White Knight is on g1.

The move **e3e2** advances the pawn to the 7th rank (for Black), threatening promotion on the next move. The pawn is supported by the Rook on e8.
- The White King on c3 cannot capture the pawn on e2.
- The White Bishop on c2 cannot capture the pawn on e2 (wrong color diagonal).
- The only piece that can capture the pawn is the White Knight on g1 (Nxe2).
- If White plays Nxe2, Black responds with Rxe2, winning a Knight for a pawn and placing the Rook on an active square.
- If White does not capture, the pawn promotes on e1, winning the game.

Therefore, pushing the pawn is the most forcing and advantageous move.

make_move e3e2

- The White Knight at d4 is attacking the Black Pawn at f5.
- If Black does not move the pawn or defend it, White will capture it on the next turn (Nxf5).
- The Black King at e7 is currently safe.
- The Black Pawn at a7 is blocked by the White Pawn at a6.

**Plan:**
- The most logical move is to advance the pawn at f5 to f4. This saves the pawn from capture by the Knight (since the Knight at d4 does not attack f4) and advances the pawn towards promotion.
- The square f4 is empty (Row 5, Col 6 is ⭘).
- The move is legal for a Black Pawn (moving from Rank 5 to Rank 4).

**Move:**
make_move f5f4

Player_Black (to Proxy):



The board shows the following position:
- **White**: King at a2, Queen at f1, Pawn at a6.
- **Black**: King at d6, Pawn at a7.

It is Black's turn to move.

**Analysis:**
1.  **Threats**: The White Pawn at a6 is blocked by the Black Pawn at a7. However, the White Pawn can capture on b7 (axb7). If White plays axb7, the pawn will be on b7, threatening to promote on b8. The Black King at d6 is not in a position to stop this promotion in time if the pawn advances (d6 -> c7 -> b8 takes 2 moves, while b7 -> b8 takes 1 move). Therefore, Black must prevent the pawn from moving to b7 or defend the b7 square.
2.  **King Safety**: The Black King is at d6. It is not currently in check. The White Queen at f1 controls the f-file, the 1st rank, and the long diagonal f1-a6. The Queen supports the pawn on a6.
3.  **Candidate Moves**: The Black King needs to move to a square that defends b7 or controls the promotion square.
    -   **Kc7**: Moves the King to c7. This square defends b7 (adjacent). If White plays axb7, Black can capture with Kxb7. It also keeps the King active and away from the White Queen's immediate checks.
    -   **Kc6**: Moves the King to c6. This also defends b7. However, c6 is on the same rank as the White Pawn (a6) and might be more exposed to checks or tactics involving the Queen.
    -   Other King moves (Ke7, Kd7, etc.) do not defend b7 adequately and would allow White to play axb7 with a winning advantage.

**Decision**:
Moving the King to c7 (**d6c7**) is the most solid move. It defends against the immediate threat of axb7 by controlling the b7 square. It also maintains the King's activity.

**Move**:
`make_move d6c7`

The position shows White's Queen on d2 and a misplaced Rook on h3. Black has a Knight on f6 and a Pawn on d5.
I see a tactical opportunity with the Knight.
The move **...Ne4** (Knight from f6 to e4) attacks the White Queen on d2.
Although the Queen is defended by the King on e1, the square e4 is controlled by the Black Pawn on d5.
Therefore, if the White Queen captures the Knight (Qxe4), she will be captured by the Pawn (dxe4), resulting in the loss of the Queen.
Consequently, the White Queen must move, allowing Black to gain a tempo and place a Knight on a very strong central outpost (e4), from where it can threaten further weaknesses (like f2).
This is the best move to punish White's passive setup.

The position is complex with multiple Queens on the board. As Black, I have a Queen on d8 and another on d1. The White King is on g2, and the White Queen is on b5. The Black Knight on c6 is currently pinned by the White Queen on b5 along the diagonal b5-e8.

I have identified a strong move: **Qd5** (moving the Queen from d8 to d5).
1.  **Check**: The Queen on d5 checks the White King on g2 along the diagonal d5-g2 (path e4, f3 is clear).
2.  **Fork**: The Queen on d5 also attacks the White Queen on b5 along the 5th rank.
3.  **Unpinning**: By forcing the White Queen to move or be captured, the pin on the Black Knight on c6 is lifted, activating the Knight.
4.  **Initiative**: Black seizes the initiative with a double attack (King and Queen). White has no good defense. If the King moves, Black captures the Queen. If the Queen moves, the King is in check and must move, allowing Black to capture the Queen or continue the attack.

Therefore, the best move is to move the Queen from d8 to d5.

Black has a Rook on f5 and a Pawn on f3.
White has a King on h3 and a Rook on h2.
The Black Pawn on f3 is supported by the Black Rook on f5.
Moving the pawn to f2 (`f3f2`) creates a strong passed pawn threat.

If White does not capture, the pawn is one step away from promotion.
If White moves the King to g3 (`Kg3`) to defend f2, Black can capture the pawn on g5 (`Rxg5`), winning material and creating further threats.

Player_Black (to Proxy):



The board shows a critical position where Black has a strong attack against the exposed White King on g3. The Black Queen is on f1, and the Black Rook is on f5. The White King is restricted by its own pawns on g4 and g5, and the Black Queen controls key squares around the King.

The best move is to deliver a check with the Rook, forcing the White King into a mating net.

1.  **Analyze the position**:
    *   Black Queen on f1 controls the f-file and diagonals (f1-h3, f1-a6).
    *   Black Rook on f5.
    *   White King on g3.
    *   White Rook on h2.
    *   White Pawns on g4, g5.
    *   Black Pawn on g6.

2.  **Candidate Move: `f5f3` (Rook f5 to f3)**:
    *   This move places the Rook on f3, delivering a check to the White King on g3 (along the 3rd rank).
    *   The White King cannot capture the Rook because it is protected by the Black Queen on f1.
    *   The White King cannot move to f2, f3, or f4 because these squares are controlled by the Black Queen on f1 (f-file and diagonals).
    *   The White King cannot move to g2 because it is controlled by the Black Queen on f1 (diagonal f1-g2).
    *   The White King cannot move to h2 because it is occupied by the White Rook.
    *   The White King cannot move to h3 because it is controlled by the Black Queen on f1 (diagonal f1-h3).
    *   The White King cannot move to g4 because it is occupied by a White Pawn.
    *   The only legal move for the White King is to **h4**.

3.  **Follow-up**:
    *   After `... Kh4`, Black can play `Qh1`, threatening `Qxh2#` (Queen captures Rook on h2, delivering checkmate).
    *   The White Rook on h2 is the only piece that can interfere, but any move by the Rook allows Black to deliver mate, typically with `Qh3#` or by capturing the Rook if it moves to a vulnerable square. For example, if White moves the Rook to a2, Black plays `Qh3#`. If White moves the Rook to h3, Black plays `Qxh3#`.

Therefore, `f5f3` is the winning move.

make_move f5f3

Player_Black (to Proxy):



The position shows that Black is down material (a Rook) and the c4 pawn is under attack by the White Rook on c2. The c-pawn is undefended and cannot be saved. Therefore, the priority is to improve the King's position and avoid immediate checks.

Moving the King to d7 (`e8d7`) is the best option because:
1.  It centralizes the King, which is generally good in endgames.
2.  It controls the c7 and c8 squares, preventing the White Rook from delivering checks on the c-file (specifically `Rc7+` and `Rc8+`) after it captures the c-pawn.
3.  Moving the c-pawn (`c4c3`) would likely result in it being captured anyway (`Rxc3`), leaving the King exposed on e8 to checks like `Rc8+`.

Thus, `e8d7` is the most resilient move.

make_move e8d7

`g6g5` advances the pawn to the 5th rank.

Losing a pawn without compensation is generally not advisable unless it leads to a forced mate or significant advantage, which doesn't seem to be the case here.
2.  **King Moves:**
    -   `e7f8`, `e7e8`, `e7d8`: These moves are passive and retreat the King to the back rank.
    -   `e7f7`, `e7d7`: Lateral moves.
    -   `e7e6`, `e7d6`: Centralizing moves.

Since the move `e7f6` is listed in the legal moves, the square f6 is not attacked by the White Queen (likely due to the specific geometry or blocking, though visually the diagonal seems open, the legality confirms safety).

Moving the King to f6 (`e7f6`) is the most active and sound move. It centralizes the King, protects the pawn on g5, and restricts the White King's movement on the f-file.

I analyzed the available squares for the knight:
- `f6d5`: This square is attacked by the White pawn on c2 (via `c4`). If I play `f6d5`, White can play `c4`, attacking the knight. Since the knight would be undefended, this would lead to the loss of the knight or a loss of tempo.
- `f6e4`: This square is attacked by the White pawn on d3. White would play `dxe4`, capturing the knight.
- `f6g4`: This square is attacked by the White Bishop on h3. White would play `Bxg4`, capturing the knight.
- `f6d7`: This square is safe, but it blocks the diagonal of the Black Bishop on c8 (which aims at h3). Blocking a developing piece is generally undesirable.
- `f6g8`: This square is safe but passive, returning the knight to its starting position.
- `f6h5`: This square is safe. It is not attacked by the pawn on g5 (which attacks f6 and h6), nor by the Bishop on h3 (which moves diagonally). It also does not block any of my pieces. Additionally, it places the knight on an active square on the kingside.

Therefore, `f6h5` is the best move. It saves the knight, maintains activity, and does not compromise my position.

Player_Black (to Proxy):



The board shows a position where Black has a pawn on d3, very close to White's King on e1. The d-file is open for the Black Queen, but blocked by the pawn on d3. Moving the pawn to d2 will clear the d-file and deliver a check to the White King.

Analysis of the move `d3d2`:
1.  **Check**: The pawn on d2 attacks c1 and e1. Since the White King is on e1, this is a check.
2.  **Forcing**: The White King must respond. The only piece that can capture the pawn on d2 is the King itself (`Kxd2`), as no other White piece controls d2.
3.  **Follow-up**: If White captures (`Kxd2`), the d-file is fully open. Black can then play `Qd4+`, delivering a check with the Queen. The White King will be exposed and under severe pressure from the Queen and the Bishop on a3 (which controls the c1-h6 diagonal and can support checks or trap the King).
4.  **Promotion Threat**: If White does not capture, the pawn is one step away from promotion on the next turn.

This move initiates a decisive attack on the White King.
